  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer6628257536224517348">One of the most influential practitioners of American horror, H.P. Lovecraft inspired the work of Stephen King, Anne Rice, and Clive Barker.   <span id="freeText6628257536224517348" style="display:none">One of the most influential practitioners of American horror, H.P. Lovecraft inspired the work of Stephen King, Anne Rice, and Clive Barker. As he perfected his mastery of the macabre, his works developed from seminal fragments into acknowledged masterpieces of terror. This volume traces his chilling career and includes:<br />IMPRISONED WITH THE PHARAOHS--Houdini seeks to reveal the demons that inhabit the Egyptian night.<br />AT THE MOUNTAINS OF MADNESS--An unsuspecting expedition uncovers a city of untold terror, buried beneath an Antarctic wasteland.<br />Plus, for the first time in any Del Rey edition:<br />HERBERT WEST: REANIMATOR--Mad experiments yield hideous results in this, the inspiration for the cult film Re-Animator.<br />COOL AIR--An icy apartment hides secrets no man dares unlock.<br />THE TERRIBLE OLD MAN--The intruders seek a fortune but find only death!<br />AND TWENTY-FOUR MORE BLOOD-CHILLING TALES</span>

<div id="review_16093053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16093053" />
    <a title="Werner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner"><img alt="Werner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16093053?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Werner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Werner" href="/user/show/903390-werner">Werner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">All fans of scary or macabre fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16093053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17269271755875129243">Note, Jan. 17, 2016: I just edited this review to correct one misspelling.<br/><br/>The selections in this volume are arranged chronologically, so that they show the progression, and gradual maturing, of Lovecraft's work, from early stories like "The Transition of Juan Romero" to his powerful later work like the novella <i>At the Mountains of Madness</i>. (This is the titular transition; the title and subtitle play on the names of both of these Lovecraft works, but aren't meant to imply that he ever went mad.) A</span>
  <span id="freeText17269271755875129243" style="display:none">Note, Jan. 17, 2016: I just edited this review to correct one misspelling.<br /><br />The selections in this volume are arranged chronologically, so that they show the progression, and gradual maturing, of Lovecraft's work, from early stories like "The Transition of Juan Romero" to his powerful later work like the novella <i>At the Mountains of Madness</i>. (This is the titular transition; the title and subtitle play on the names of both of these Lovecraft works, but aren't meant to imply that he ever went mad.) All of the material here effectively displays the same characteristics I noted in my earlier review of <i>The Best of H. P. Lovecraft</i>, which is part of the same Del Rey series.<br /><br />Stories like "Arthur Jermyn" (titled "White Ape" in some anthologies) and "The Lurking Fear" vividly demonstrate the hag-ridden fear of devolution that lies at the heart of Darwinism. (The Darwinism of Lovecraft's day was also openly racist, which accounts for the racially-insensitive language in parts of Lovecraft's corpus that Hambly notes in her introduction; but her charge of sexism is less credible. Lovecraft wrote almost entirely about male characters because, apart from his short-lived marriage, he had little contact with women other than his mother and his aunts and had little understanding of them; but that isn't the same thing as sexism.) "The White Ship" illustrates the vein of mystical, even beautiful fantasy that rarely appears in his work; while "Imprisoned With the Pharaohs" (written with Harry Houdini) is a good example of his many collaborations, in which he took someone's basic idea and worked it up in his own inimitable style. Besides these, some of my own personal favorites in this volume include "The Shunned House," "The Horror at Red Hook," "The Unnameable," "Cool Air," "The Moon-Bog," and "Herbert West --Reanimator." But ALL of them are well worth a read! </span>
  <a data-text-id="17269271755875129243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2191342585" />
    <a title="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62203305-sumi-s-books"><img alt="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580352861p2/62203305.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" class="user" itemprop="url" name="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" href="/user/show/62203305-sumi-s-books">✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2191342585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3979462459362173635">This is a compilation of HP Lovecraft's works. This one includes a few of his early tales along with Dagon, Imprisoned with the Pharaohs, and At the Mountains of Madness. His works are definitely a favorite of mine and I would recommend this to anyone.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_151188530" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151188530" />
    <a title="Kevin Hinman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4369248-kevin-hinman"><img alt="Kevin Hinman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525535288p2/4369248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151188530?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Hinman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Hinman" href="/user/show/4369248-kevin-hinman">Kevin Hinman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151188530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2659496325068300284">The Transition of H. P. Lovecraft is a fairly solid group of shorts, but isn't really recommended for those new to the macabre world of Lovecraft. The collection favors more obscure early pieces, which are interesting as curios for completests, but since they were written when H. P. was still sulking around school and listening to whatever the 1900s version of The Cure was, most of them are garbage.<br/><br/>The standout works are the wonderfully crafted Mountains of Madness, Herbert West: Re-animator, In</span>
  <span id="freeText2659496325068300284" style="display:none">The Transition of H. P. Lovecraft is a fairly solid group of shorts, but isn't really recommended for those new to the macabre world of Lovecraft. The collection favors more obscure early pieces, which are interesting as curios for completests, but since they were written when H. P. was still sulking around school and listening to whatever the 1900s version of The Cure was, most of them are garbage.<br /><br />The standout works are the wonderfully crafted Mountains of Madness, Herbert West: Re-animator, In the Walls of Eryx, and my personal favorite - The Temple, in which a U-Boat captain slowly goes insane after his vessel sinks down to the ruins of a lost city.<br /><br />These stories are all fantastic, haunting Gothic tales that rival the best Poe, but for every Re-animator there is an Evil Clergyman and a Beast in the Cave to bookend it. Fans, come back for your favorites and explore the Juvenilia at your own risk. First timers, pick up a Best Of anthology instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2659496325068300284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12159522689257249269" style="display:none">Reading Lovecraft is like having a horrific tale told to you by an english gentleman in a waist coat over tea in a parlor with a roaring fire. It's like sitting in front of the hearth in a large wing back chair while the storm rages outside. I now understand why Stephen King credits him as an inspiration. I'm told that this volume is far from his best, I'm all a flutter to get my hands on the next volume. I also understand that he wrote many of these stories as a fifteen year old. The fact alone that they are flawless in grammar and structure, but the ability to create a lasting work of fantasy at that age. A lovely collection of mayhem!</span>

<div id="review_445595776" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445595776" />
    <a title="Fiza Pathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13975238-fiza-pathan"><img alt="Fiza Pathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506336980p2/13975238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445595776?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fiza Pathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fiza Pathan" href="/user/show/13975238-fiza-pathan">Fiza Pathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445595776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6434827093237242283">I must admit that this was the first time I actually came across the author H. P. Lovecraft. I found this book at the Strand Bookstall in Mumbai after it fell on my head as I was rummaging through a bookshelf containing some other great books on philosophy. <br/><br/>I was taken aback when I read on the back cover of the book that Lovecraft had inspired many of my own favourite authors of the macabre like Anne Rice, Stephen King & Clive Barker. As I poured over the stories at night UNDER my study table wi</span>
  <span id="freeText6434827093237242283" style="display:none">I must admit that this was the first time I actually came across the author H. P. Lovecraft. I found this book at the Strand Bookstall in Mumbai after it fell on my head as I was rummaging through a bookshelf containing some other great books on philosophy. <br /><br />I was taken aback when I read on the back cover of the book that Lovecraft had inspired many of my own favourite authors of the macabre like Anne Rice, Stephen King &amp; Clive Barker. As I poured over the stories at night UNDER my study table with the table lamp on, I was transported to a realm quite different from my own understanding of terror &amp; fantasy. Lovecraft's ideas were not only macabre but also quite morbid &amp; blood chilling. True that his works are steeped in grand descriptions which normally puts a casual reader of, but a true lover of literature &amp; horror will certainly realize after reading Lovecraft's works like 'At The Mountains Of Madness', 'Reanimator', 'Imprisoned With The Pharaohs' etc that, the descriptions are all meaningful to completely realize the actual horror behind it all. <br /><br />As Barbara Hambly stated, H.P. Lovecraft struggles to bring out his ideas to the reader....it is his idea that is powerful &amp; extraordinary. His pseudo - Poe short stories take on another turn as they get more original &amp; more bizzare as the years go by. Lovecraft's characters too keep on undergoing transformations &amp; at times reappear in other stories. He in the bargain creates a niche for himself in the horror &amp; fantasy genre which no one can rob. <br /><br />He is excellent as I have observed in first person accounts of the story, making the reader grip the book tightly in a cold sweat as he administers the opiate of fear into our system....almost like his warped character Herbert West does by administering a special powder into the veins of dead bodies or organs to bring them to life in the 'Reanimator'(this was better than Frankenstein). Most of Lovecraft's protagonists I have observed are men (its always men !!!) who are :<br /><br />1] Well educated<br /><br />2] Intelligent<br /><br />3] Believe in the dark forces &amp; works of very sinister personalities (eg., the constant repition of the book Necronomicon by the Arab Abdul Alhazred who was a genius par excellance)<br /><br />4] Have this constant habit of getting into trouble inspite of their intelligence <br /><br />No doubt that at times his stories have a certain amount of Racism present in it, never the less, Lovecraft still pens his stories with a masterstroke of a true wizard of the terrible. Most of his main characters as I have stated before are common scholars or scientists except for 'Imprisoned With The Pharoahs' where the poor person imprisoned is the world famous escape artist (got to love him) Harry Houdini. <br /><br />My personal favourites in this book were ;<br /><br />1) The Transition Of Juan Romero<br /><br />2) The Temple<br /><br />3) The Terrible Old Man<br /><br />4) Reanimator (lots of gross descriptions &amp; lots of blood....too good !)<br /><br />5) Imprisoned With The Pharaohs (I love Houdini)<br /><br />6) The Horror At Red Hook (out of this world !) &amp;<br /><br />7) In The Walls Of Eryx (he co -authored this with Kenneth Sterling)<br /><br />Lovecraft refers a lot to Edgar Allen Poe in most of his early works especially in 'The Shunned House' where it looks like he really was enamoured by the original creator of the macabre. <br /><br />All in all.......a thumbs up for Lovecraft &amp; his 'madness'. Now I am going to check out all the movies that have been based on this stories. Indeed, many movies have found their genesis in the works of this master of ghastly descriptions. <br /><br />I shall post some more information &amp; links after I finish my research. Got to get my students to check this guy out.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6434827093237242283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26854431" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26854431" />
    <a title="Hunter Duesing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1314395-hunter-duesing"><img alt="Hunter Duesing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1215647315p2/1314395.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26854431?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hunter Duesing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hunter Duesing" href="/user/show/1314395-hunter-duesing">Hunter Duesing</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1314395-hunter-duesing?shelf=short-story-collections">short-story-collections</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1314395-hunter-duesing?shelf=wierd-fiction">wierd-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26854431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10666531039224324857">What I like about thes collections of Lovecraft's work is that are themed in some way. The purpose of this collection is to track Lovecraft's evolution as a writer. As Neil Gaiman is quick to point out in his introduction, most of Lovecraft's early work (like 'The Beast in the Cave' for example) isn't very good, but it shows promise, and as this book proves, that promise was fulfilled as Lovecraft grew as a writer. If you are new to Lovecraft's short fiction, you'll probably want to start elsewh</span>
  <span id="freeText10666531039224324857" style="display:none">What I like about thes collections of Lovecraft's work is that are themed in some way. The purpose of this collection is to track Lovecraft's evolution as a writer. As Neil Gaiman is quick to point out in his introduction, most of Lovecraft's early work (like 'The Beast in the Cave' for example) isn't very good, but it shows promise, and as this book proves, that promise was fulfilled as Lovecraft grew as a writer. If you are new to Lovecraft's short fiction, you'll probably want to start elsewhere by picking up a "greatest hits" collection of his work. If you've read Lovecraft and you enjoy what he has to offer, you'll find a great deal of juicy stuff to sink your teeth into.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10666531039224324857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review26854431">

<div id="review_47304601" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47304601" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2064885-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1235438694p2/2064885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47304601?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/2064885-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lovecraft fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2064885-jonathan?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47304601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3809065365207421319"> What does it mean to be mad? One might define madness as the state wherein one can no longer distinguish between real, objective phenomena, as experienced empirically by the senses, from certain delusions of the mind; seeing ghosts, as it were. But surely the madman believes the phantasms of his mind to be quite real; this is why the objective standard of empiricism is indispensible. But what if one’s senses convey something which all one’s prior experience betrays as logically impossible? Does</span>
  <span id="freeText3809065365207421319" style="display:none"> What does it mean to be mad? One might define madness as the state wherein one can no longer distinguish between real, objective phenomena, as experienced empirically by the senses, from certain delusions of the mind; seeing ghosts, as it were. But surely the madman believes the phantasms of his mind to be quite real; this is why the objective standard of empiricism is indispensible. But what if one’s senses convey something which all one’s prior experience betrays as logically impossible? Does one trust to empiricism, or, in true Kantian fashion, shape reality according to his experience? Of the two paths, rejecting what one experiences or believing something contrary to one’s logical makeup, which is true madness? This issue goes right to the heart of many of the stories of H.P. Lovecraft, assembled in this collection appropriately titled <i>The Road to Madness</i>.<br /><br /> Lovecraft is most famous for his Cthulu mythos cycle, but only a few of the stories here can be said to belong to the mythos proper. The rest are simply tales of men who have glimpsed a horrible secret, or carry that secret within themselves, and as a result teeter on the edge of madness. Take, for instance, Arthur Jermyn, ill-fated hero of the story of the same name. Research into his ancestral history hints at a monstrous pedigree, and a final damning piece of evidence tips the scales; Arthur is driven to madness and suicide. The story is exemplary of Lovecraft’s classical <i>modus operandi</i>; the protagonist, most often a first-person narrator, passes off as coincidence or delusion events which the reader knows are meant to be taken as actual occurrences, until some climactic circumstance forces him to confront what he had previously only dared to suspect. Those who are not, like Jermyn, driven over the edge will attribute their horrible experiences to mental fatigue, nervousness, or an overactive imagination. The fact remains, however, that the reader knows that what has happened was indeed real.<br /><br /> In his mythos stories, the horrible reality lurking beneath the superficiality of human understanding connects to the very heart of the universe and the other beings which inhabit it. For example, in <i>At the Mountains of Madness</i>, one of Lovecraft’s most famous works, an expedition to the Antarctic uncovers the remains of a city built long before humans walked the earth. Shaken, but with sanity intact, the survivors resolve to keep the horrible secret from the rest of the world. In other stories, however, the horror arises from other sources. In <i>Herbert West – Reanimator</i>, the titular character learns the secret of restoring dead tissue to a semblance of life. Far from being deterred by the monstrous results, West pushes further still until his macabre obsession literally consumes him. <br /><br /> The collection is not completely filled with darkness and horror, however. Included are a sample of Lovecraft’s earliest works, which include some clumsy imitations of Poe, but show the maturation of a writer slowly finding his own voice. There are several short pieces and poems which show Lovecraft’s command of language to create a unique aesthetic. <i>The Tree</i> is one such example, a short fable set in ancient Greece. One of the most interesting offerings is <i>In the Walls of Eryx</i>, which is pure science-fiction. Framed as the recordings of a field worker sent on a resource-gathering expedition on Venus, Lovecraft’s trademark psychological musing is again at work, only here the confounding device is of alien, rather than supernatural origin.<br /><br /> Because the stories in this collection focus mostly on the mental battles of the characters, and because only a few of the more well-known works are included, I wouldn’t necessarily recommend it as an introduction to Lovecraft. But for those familiar with his work, or who don’t mind taking a chance on some eldritch mystery, there is much here to incite the mind and imagination.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3809065365207421319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_303748074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303748074" />
    <a title="Traummachine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8495289-traummachine"><img alt="Traummachine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357275046p2/8495289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/303748074?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Traummachine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Traummachine" href="/user/show/8495289-traummachine">Traummachine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer303748074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10653591830368952028">3.5 stars (as a collection):<br/>It's hard to rate a Lovecraft collection. He is pretty hit and miss, so most of his collections are probably mixed bags. But all of them I've read contain several stories that are Lovecraft at his best. So I guess the best thing to do is to briefly cover my faves so you can decide if this is a good collection for you to pick up. Keep in mind that some of these might seem cliche, but that's due to all the imitators. Only Poe and a handful of others were writing Horror </span>
  <span id="freeText10653591830368952028" style="display:none">3.5 stars (as a collection):<br />It's hard to rate a Lovecraft collection. He is pretty hit and miss, so most of his collections are probably mixed bags. But all of them I've read contain several stories that are Lovecraft at his best. So I guess the best thing to do is to briefly cover my faves so you can decide if this is a good collection for you to pick up. Keep in mind that some of these might seem cliche, but that's due to all the imitators. Only Poe and a handful of others were writing Horror before Lovecraft.<br /><br /><u>The Terrible Old Man:</u> Easily the best 3 page story I've ever read. Despite the super short length, when I start listing my favorite Lovecraft tales this is always one of the first to come to mind. It's so short that if I say anything else about it I'll give it away!<br /><br /><u>Herbert West--Reanimator:</u> Take the movie <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0089885/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Re-Animator</a>, and make it more <i>Night</i> and less <i>Return</i> (of the Living Dead) and you've got a pretty good idea. Very cool, easily the earliest zombie story I've ever read.<br /><br /><u>The Lurking Fear:</u> <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0110410/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Link to the movie, which I saw a long time ago</a>. A town has been plagued for decades by ghoulish creatures which appear and then mysteriously vanish. Our hero discovers the subterranean tunnels they use and explores them. Very tense (since the hero's an academic, not a Rambo)!<br /><br /><u>The Unnamable:</u> <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0096344/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Not a good movie</a>, but it is a very good short story. An author tries to convince his friend that his horror stories are not so far-fetched as they might seem.<br /><br /><u>Cool Air:</u> <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0780495/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">A good adaptation of Lovecraft</a>. Our hero moves into a tenement, where he is saved from a heart attack by the mysterious doctor who lives upstairs from him. He and the doctor start to become friends, but the strange smells, sounds, and temperature in his apartment make him feel more and more uneasy.<br /><br /><u>At the Mountains of Madness:</u> <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0768216/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">I have no idea how this movie is</a>. But the story is a milestone for Lovecraft -- one of his 3 longest tales, it describes an expedition into Antarctica, where strange creatures are dug up from the ice, where advance parties of the expedition are found slaughtered, and where horrible ancient civilizations are found and explored. This definitely influenced one of my favorite movies of all time, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0084787/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Thing</a>.<br /><br />There are several other solid Lovecraft stories in this collection, but there are just as many that were blah. So the good stories here are amazing, but as a collection it's just meh for non-completists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10653591830368952028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_129816936" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129816936" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2224950-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428875161p2/2224950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129816936?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/2224950-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=2000">2000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=reread-books">reread-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129816936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8048102816534054146">First Recorded Reading: October 9, 2000<br/><br/><br/>In my continuing quest for interesting bedtime reading, I have finished this cheerful volume of dark tales from H. P. Lovecraft. (I have three of these volumes, all with very creepy cover art by surreal artist John Jude Palencar; this volume, <i>The Best of H. P. Lovecraft: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre</i> by H. P. Lovecraft, Introduction by Robert Bloch, and <i>The Dream Cycle of H. P. Lovecraft: Dreams of Terror and Death</i> by H. P. Lovecraft, Introd</span>
  <span id="freeText8048102816534054146" style="display:none">First Recorded Reading: October 9, 2000<br /><br /><br />In my continuing quest for interesting bedtime reading, I have finished this cheerful volume of dark tales from H. P. Lovecraft. (I have three of these volumes, all with very creepy cover art by surreal artist John Jude Palencar; this volume, <i>The Best of H. P. Lovecraft: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre</i> by H. P. Lovecraft, Introduction by Robert Bloch, and <i>The Dream Cycle of H. P. Lovecraft: Dreams of Terror and Death</i> by H. P. Lovecraft, Introduction by Neil Gaiman. And I should note that I am apparently reading these volumes out of order, because this present book contains a lot of material from the author’s early work, before he became a master horror writer. But this volume is a very good one, with some great material, and I recommend it to those who love the craft of Lovecraft.<br /><br />The book begins with five early tales, followed by a fragment of a story; then the collection swings into high gear, with such wonderful stories as “The White Ship”, “The Crawling Chaos” (no one could beat Lovecraft for great story names), “The Lurking Fear”, “Imprisoned with the Pharaohs” (ghost-written by Lovecraft for Harry Houdini, who claimed that the incident recorded had happened to him; since Houdini’s account turned out to be mostly fictitious, Lovecraft got to let his imagination loose on the story), “The Shunned House” (which makes no sense whatsoever, upon reflection, but is still a great story), and “Cool Air”.<br /><br />The novella “At The Mountains of Madness” is in this collection; and besides being a story that tells us You Really Don’t Want To Know What Is At Antarctica, I wish that Professor Dyer could use his powers of interpreting mural art in terms of both time and space to more accessible art (like Egyptian murals). The collection ends with two short stories, one of which, “In The Walls of Eryx”, is an out-and-out science fiction story, set on the planet Venus.<br /><br />I think this is a great collection of Lovecraft’s stories, and I look forward to re-reading <i>The Dream Cycle of H. P. Lovecraft: Dreams of Terror and Death</i> by H. P. Lovecraft, Introduction by Neil Gaiman as my bedtime reading for a week or so.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8048102816534054146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1669267195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1669267195" />
    <a title="Leothefox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41492247-leothefox"><img alt="Leothefox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427161472p2/41492247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1669267195?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leothefox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leothefox" href="/user/show/41492247-leothefox">Leothefox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1669267195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5055284525764375158">This volume is sort of a history, taking us along the chronological road of Lovecraft's development. We start with the very early “The Beast in the Cave” and into some curious efforts on our way up through “Dagon”, “The White Ship”, and one of my all-time favorites, “The Crawling Chaos”. Even then, it's just getting started.<br/><br/>	Even for a seasoned Lovecraft enthusiast, this makes for one weird and interesting trip! Some of it is fanciful strangeness that almost doesn't connect up to anything, other</span>
  <span id="freeText5055284525764375158" style="display:none">This volume is sort of a history, taking us along the chronological road of Lovecraft's development. We start with the very early “The Beast in the Cave” and into some curious efforts on our way up through “Dagon”, “The White Ship”, and one of my all-time favorites, “The Crawling Chaos”. Even then, it's just getting started.<br /><br />	Even for a seasoned Lovecraft enthusiast, this makes for one weird and interesting trip! Some of it is fanciful strangeness that almost doesn't connect up to anything, other stuff drifts into politics and points elsewhere. Here we find a WW1 horror story, a tale set on Venus, and a Harry Houdini adventure! <br /><br />	For more famous tales, there's “Herbert West-Reanimator”, “At the Mountains of Madness”, “The Shunned House” and “The Lurking Fear”. There's also a story I've always found memorable but which I never heard anybody mention, “The Alchemist”, which involves a narrator doomed by a family curse. <br /><br />	There are stories from the New York period, “The Horror at Red Hook”, “Cool Air”, and the ultimate rejection of the modern metropolis, “He”. Lovecraft's xenophobia fires there stories along, channeling popular fears of the day which are somehow not gone yet. <br /><br />	There are 29 stories here, providing a rich variety, strengthened by the wealth of short pieces sandwiched in with meatier ones like “Herbert West” and “At the Mountains of Madness”. Some of these tales are like old friends by now, especially “The Crawling Chaos” and “The Festival” (seriously, read this one if nothing else!). The word “nightmare” is overused when describing horror fiction, but Lovecraft really nails the dream state, right up to the ripe cherry at the very end, “The Evil Clergyman”. <br /><br />	Read it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5055284525764375158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Martin Gibbs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6155314-martin-gibbs"><img alt="Martin Gibbs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442619052p2/6155314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/258914997?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martin Gibbs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martin Gibbs" href="/user/show/6155314-martin-gibbs">Martin Gibbs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6155314-martin-gibbs?shelf=best-literature">best-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer258914997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15990990215048879654">Purple<br/><br/><br/>Purple is the word that comes to mind after reading this collection of stories from one of the original masters. I think of the purple void of nightmare and the purple prose that wends its way through these passages. The writing is terrific, dark, brooding, flowing—but sometimes you can fee strangled by it, wrapped in its stream.<br/><br/>The stories themselves are great, the masterpiece in my opinion being "At the Mountains of Madness." But, through all of the blocks of text, with all the deep desc</span>
  <span id="freeText15990990215048879654" style="display:none">Purple<br /><br /><br />Purple is the word that comes to mind after reading this collection of stories from one of the original masters. I think of the purple void of nightmare and the purple prose that wends its way through these passages. The writing is terrific, dark, brooding, flowing—but sometimes you can fee strangled by it, wrapped in its stream.<br /><br />The stories themselves are great, the masterpiece in my opinion being "At the Mountains of Madness." But, through all of the blocks of text, with all the deep descriptions of terror and the Old Ones, there is no dialogue. After reading de Camp's biography, I realize Lovecraft was averse to the stuff, but to quote a famous football player "C'mon, man!" You can still build terror and fear though the speech of the characters.<br /><br />This is a great volume for those wishing to go back and see the inspiration for a century of horror and fantasy. Video games, novels, movies, etc., have all drawn from these tales. It is fun to go back and read the original—for audiences in the '30s, this was some seriously scary stuff.<br /><br />Three stars for lack of dialogue and a lot of purple. But purple's not a bad color.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15990990215048879654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15894961065586965644">I really enjoyed this book. There isn't much I can say about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9494.H_P__Lovecraft' title='H.P. Lovecraft' rel='nofollow noopener'>H.P. Lovecraft</a> that hasn't already been said a bajillion times, but I can take a stab at it.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36317.The_Road_to_Madness' title='The Road to Madness by H.P. Lovecraft' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Road to Madness</a> is a collection of Lovecraft's stories, but it feels like the collection's quality is hit-or-miss. Some of the earlier works are there, and are fun to read, but when you look at some stories, they are clearly better than others. However, this does give a good insight into some of the progress that Lovecraft made as an author,</span>
  <span id="freeText15894961065586965644" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book. There isn't much I can say about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9494.H_P__Lovecraft" title="H.P. Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">H.P. Lovecraft</a> that hasn't already been said a bajillion times, but I can take a stab at it.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36317.The_Road_to_Madness" title="The Road to Madness by H.P. Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road to Madness</a> is a collection of Lovecraft's stories, but it feels like the collection's quality is hit-or-miss. Some of the earlier works are there, and are fun to read, but when you look at some stories, they are clearly better than others. However, this does give a good insight into some of the progress that Lovecraft made as an author, and the changes his style underwent.<br /><br />That being said, I don't think I'd read it again. For one, the cover art is really freaky, and I'm worried that it'd give my kids nightmares. The stories are good ones--and ones you see less frequently that others--but there are certainly better collections out there.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13302050189429816017">In The Transition of H.P. Lovecraft: The Road to Madness, I read the short story He. It is about this young man who hates walking during the day so he walks around at night. He does this every night until one night he meets this old man. He's wearing dark old clothes with a trench coat. The old man tells the young man to follow him, so he did and he followed him for a very long time in the dark. They continued walking until they both get to the old mans house and they go inside. Then the old man</span>
  <span id="freeText13302050189429816017" style="display:none">In The Transition of H.P. Lovecraft: The Road to Madness, I read the short story He. It is about this young man who hates walking during the day so he walks around at night. He does this every night until one night he meets this old man. He's wearing dark old clothes with a trench coat. The old man tells the young man to follow him, so he did and he followed him for a very long time in the dark. They continued walking until they both get to the old mans house and they go inside. Then the old man brings the young man up stairs into a room.<br />He lights a candle and starts telling the young man scary stories. Then the old man tells the young man to look outside the window. So he does and then the old man starts chanting and then the young man starts seeing very demonic things. The young man falls to the ground and starts screaming and the old man starts getting really mad at the young man and scratches him with his long old nails. Then the old man starts getting really scared. The young man realizes this and asks why are you getting scared. And he says they're coming for me. <br />I liked that the book was based a really long time ago when New York was first getting built. What I didn't like was that they spoke so different back then so it was hard to comprehend and read but it was a good read overall. I would recommend this to anyone who likes a suspenseful and kind of scary book. </span>

<div id="review_2264457857" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2264457857" />
    <a title="luciddreamer99" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8432851-luciddreamer99"><img alt="luciddreamer99" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335847385p2/8432851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2264457857?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="luciddreamer99" class="user" itemprop="url" name="luciddreamer99" href="/user/show/8432851-luciddreamer99">luciddreamer99</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2264457857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10002198709706878655">While the stories here are not as good as the Best of Lovecraft volume by the same publisher, this volume still has some Lovecraft goodness. Highlighted perhaps by the short novel "At the Mountains of Madness," the stories display Lovecraft's characteristically detailed prose with his brand of mysterious horror. "In the Walls of Eryx" is a nice little science fiction story I don't remember reading, and there are other great multi-part stories, such as "Herbert West--Reanimator." Overall, I'd say</span>
  <span id="freeText10002198709706878655" style="display:none">While the stories here are not as good as the Best of Lovecraft volume by the same publisher, this volume still has some Lovecraft goodness. Highlighted perhaps by the short novel "At the Mountains of Madness," the stories display Lovecraft's characteristically detailed prose with his brand of mysterious horror. "In the Walls of Eryx" is a nice little science fiction story I don't remember reading, and there are other great multi-part stories, such as "Herbert West--Reanimator." Overall, I'd say this is a good collection of Lovecraft stories, though maybe not the best. I really liked it, but I'm a big fan of Lovecraft. If you are looking for the best Lovecraft, and have never read him, I'd say check out The Best of H.P. Lovecraft: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre from Ballantine/Del Rey. This volume has some early works of the author's that, while interesting in the overall picture of his work, are just not as polished as his later works. Again, still enjoyable, still good horror, just not the best of the best. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10002198709706878655" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2264457857">

<div id="review_191046952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/191046952" />
    <a title="Nicholas Griffith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2233651-nicholas-griffith"><img alt="Nicholas Griffith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327143070p2/2233651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/191046952?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Griffith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Griffith" href="/user/show/2233651-nicholas-griffith">Nicholas Griffith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer191046952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer335524568000612409">You're almost obligated to read Lovecraft at some point if you're an avid reader. As the American father of horror he posthumously enjoys the same celebrity status as every eccentric way-paver. His accolades notwithstanding, I suspect it will be a long time before I ever pick up another of his books; his writing was to literature what J.J. Abrams' directing was to the last dozen episodes of Lost: forever arousing curiosity with nothing to show for it (though Abrams is plainly better at his craft</span>
  <span id="freeText335524568000612409" style="display:none">You're almost obligated to read Lovecraft at some point if you're an avid reader. As the American father of horror he posthumously enjoys the same celebrity status as every eccentric way-paver. His accolades notwithstanding, I suspect it will be a long time before I ever pick up another of his books; his writing was to literature what J.J. Abrams' directing was to the last dozen episodes of Lost: forever arousing curiosity with nothing to show for it (though Abrams is plainly better at his craft). As I am constantly reminded by my brother, Lovecraft was not a writer, he was an imaginer, and probably didn't have much of a handle on what he was writing himself. That being said, his writing is atrocious, and after getting through "The Mountains of Madness" in this collection I want to stab the dead imaginer for schlogging me through countless hours of glorious mirages with nothing behind them but more flickerings in the distance. So from the bottom of my heart: Fuck you Lovecraft; you beautiful goddamned weirdo...<br /><br />...who am I kidding? I'll probably be reading him again before the year is over.</span>
  <a data-text-id="335524568000612409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1378549828" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1378549828" />
    <a title="Elias" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33934236-elias"><img alt="Elias" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408105215p2/33934236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1378549828?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elias" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elias" href="/user/show/33934236-elias">Elias</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1378549828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14608625106012401687">Great stuff, I read it mostly for Mountains of Madness, but it's full of other tales. I'd had trouble finding it in other collections, such as the ST Joshi edited one with footnotes. HPL is classic, gold standard stuff for mood, and it's amazing that really what we're reading is sci-fi and not fantasy, exactly. MoM takes some diving in, as HPL is very dense with descriptions in an academic style rather than what other authors might do with popular fiction: virtually no dialogue, all descriptions</span>
  <span id="freeText14608625106012401687" style="display:none">Great stuff, I read it mostly for Mountains of Madness, but it's full of other tales. I'd had trouble finding it in other collections, such as the ST Joshi edited one with footnotes. HPL is classic, gold standard stuff for mood, and it's amazing that really what we're reading is sci-fi and not fantasy, exactly. MoM takes some diving in, as HPL is very dense with descriptions in an academic style rather than what other authors might do with popular fiction: virtually no dialogue, all descriptions, and you infer things broadly. My complaint is that he does a lot of exposition with this, following the history of certain beings rather than telling a Robert E Howard style action tale, but that's the author, and something of the point: mortal men, not heroes, are encountering the weird. Great stuff, despite its historic period being apparent; in fact, I like the immersion of that. The cover of the edition is also great fun, and is possibly the best looking version of his tales that I've come across (imho).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14608625106012401687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103706922" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103706922" />
    <a title="Nicholas Beck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3734264-nicholas-beck"><img alt="Nicholas Beck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442492681p2/3734264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103706922?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Beck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Beck" href="/user/show/3734264-nicholas-beck">Nicholas Beck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103706922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6572858599668537168">The Transition of H. P. Lovecraft was a fantastic example of the many different styles in which Lovecraft wrote his stories over the years. This contained a number of terrifically scary stories with tremendous settings outside the traditional New England location. Some of my favorites were At The Mountains Of Madness, In The Walls Of Eryx, Cool Air, The Lurking Fear, and Herbert West-Reanimator. This collection of stories took the reader to the ends of the earth, to other parts of the solar syst</span>
  <span id="freeText6572858599668537168" style="display:none">The Transition of H. P. Lovecraft was a fantastic example of the many different styles in which Lovecraft wrote his stories over the years. This contained a number of terrifically scary stories with tremendous settings outside the traditional New England location. Some of my favorites were At The Mountains Of Madness, In The Walls Of Eryx, Cool Air, The Lurking Fear, and Herbert West-Reanimator. This collection of stories took the reader to the ends of the earth, to other parts of the solar system, and to the limits which the human mind can change the world around. As with all of Lovecraft's stories the reader is left thinking about the implications the writer suggests in the incredibly horrific images.<br /><br />I would definitely read this collection of stories again, but if a reader is new to Lovecraft this is not the book with which to begin. Some of the stories are during his younger years and the difference can easily be seen in the writing as well as the complexity of the stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6572858599668537168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_558715680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558715680" />
    <a title="Ethan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/812997-ethan"><img alt="Ethan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391057428p2/812997.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558715680?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ethan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ethan" href="/user/show/812997-ethan">Ethan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/812997-ethan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558715680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13293932249095233755">This book contains some of Lovecraft's lesser-known and earlier work as well as a few famous pieces. Some of the earlier stories are only okay, but true Lovecraft fans will enjoy seeing how he progressed. Since this collection contains my all-time favorite Lovecraft story (or novella, actually), <i>At the Mountains of Madness</i>, I can't help but give it five stars. It also contains a few classics, like "Herbert West - Reanimator," and some interesting stories, like "Imprisoned with the Pharaohs" (gho</span>
  <span id="freeText13293932249095233755" style="display:none">This book contains some of Lovecraft's lesser-known and earlier work as well as a few famous pieces. Some of the earlier stories are only okay, but true Lovecraft fans will enjoy seeing how he progressed. Since this collection contains my all-time favorite Lovecraft story (or novella, actually), <i>At the Mountains of Madness</i>, I can't help but give it five stars. It also contains a few classics, like "Herbert West - Reanimator," and some interesting stories, like "Imprisoned with the Pharaohs" (ghost-written for Harry Houdini). My favorite of the less popular stories is "In the Walls of Eryx," which is old school science fiction story in an eldritch, Lovecraftian key. Barbara Hambley's introduction is surprisingly fun for a Lovecraft introduction (I suppose Lovecraft fans have to make light of all that noisome terror to stay sane). Lastly, John Jude Palencar's illustrations are delightfully devious. I only wish there had been more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13293932249095233755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_122643419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/122643419" />
    <a title="Alex Barclay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4214377-alex-barclay"><img alt="Alex Barclay" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/122643419?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer122643419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4019178197429948458">In high school, a friend of mine had a tattered copy of "The Road to Madness" that he let me borrow. Before then, I had been a big fan of Stephen King and Clive Barker, but it never occurred to me that there may be a writer more influential in my horror writing. That's when H.P. Lovecraft entered my life. Works out of this book, such as "At the Mountains of Madness", "The Horror at Red Hook", and "Herbert West, Reanimator", remain some of my favorites to this day. H.P. Lovecraft taught me to rea</span>
  <span id="freeText4019178197429948458" style="display:none">In high school, a friend of mine had a tattered copy of "The Road to Madness" that he let me borrow. Before then, I had been a big fan of Stephen King and Clive Barker, but it never occurred to me that there may be a writer more influential in my horror writing. That's when H.P. Lovecraft entered my life. Works out of this book, such as "At the Mountains of Madness", "The Horror at Red Hook", and "Herbert West, Reanimator", remain some of my favorites to this day. H.P. Lovecraft taught me to reach beyond what I considered weird, and to plumb the depths of what could be out there to find true horror. Although some of the work may seem a bit outdated, the tales of madness that Lovecraft created, to me, rival that of any horror writer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4019178197429948458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1928557335" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1928557335" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5907563-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer16137470447705514919">A kind of “sequel” to the cornerstone DelRay collection Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre, The Road to Madness is an odd hodgepodge of some of Lovecraft’s best along his very worst. Like the loose Dream Cycle stories, these include some fragments and juvenilia that apes Poe or Dunsany. However, the collection includes a few absolute essentials: At the Mountains of Madness, Herbert West - Reanimator, Dagon, Cool Air, and The Lurking Fear are all worthy entries in the Lovecraft library</span>
  <span id="freeText16137470447705514919" style="display:none">A kind of “sequel” to the cornerstone DelRay collection Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre, The Road to Madness is an odd hodgepodge of some of Lovecraft’s best along his very worst. Like the loose Dream Cycle stories, these include some fragments and juvenilia that apes Poe or Dunsany. However, the collection includes a few absolute essentials: At the Mountains of Madness, Herbert West - Reanimator, Dagon, Cool Air, and The Lurking Fear are all worthy entries in the Lovecraft library. Some of the others, like Lovecraft’s “most sci-fi” tale In the Walls of Eryx, are interesting side roads. Overall, you can pick and choose a few choice cuts with this one, unless you’re an obsessive completist like me. In that case, enjoy!</span>

<div id="review_3207791419" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3207791419" />
    <a title="Elizabeth R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7153086-elizabeth-r"><img alt="Elizabeth R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1323537804p2/7153086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3207791419?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth R." href="/user/show/7153086-elizabeth-r">Elizabeth R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3207791419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11855510576447498605">I’ve been reading, and re-reading, Lovecraft short stories recently, and finding the experience very different from what I thought I remembered of the works by the author of the Cthulhu mythos. I hadn’t previously noticed the dramatic racism that seems to taint almost every story – possibly partly because at the time I wrote such things off as mere foibles of the era, like reading older stories where people thought the earth was flat, or the sun went around the earth, or some such. <br/><br/>I guess what </span>
  <span id="freeText11855510576447498605" style="display:none">I’ve been reading, and re-reading, Lovecraft short stories recently, and finding the experience very different from what I thought I remembered of the works by the author of the Cthulhu mythos. I hadn’t previously noticed the dramatic racism that seems to taint almost every story – possibly partly because at the time I wrote such things off as mere foibles of the era, like reading older stories where people thought the earth was flat, or the sun went around the earth, or some such. <br /><br />I guess what I’m trying to say is, if you have fond memories of Cthulhu-horror stories, and don’t want to be disillusioned just yet, maybe don’t read this book.<br /><br />I found Hambly’s introduction charming in its frank admission of Lovecraft’s deeply problematic racism. In a very readable couple of pages, she introduces his style and some of its effect on popular culture, gives a little context to each of the stories, and otherwise provides an articulate and contemporary launching point for new and returning HPL readers alike.<br /><br />Unlike many short story collections, this one opens with some youthful works, pieces written in his late teens mostly. From the first of them, The Cave, to the last story, The Evil Clergyman, the journey is fairly direct, or at least the landscape feels fairly contiguous: themes of bad blood, whether human or otherwise (as a New Yorker I found his treatment of the Catskills particularly amusing); unimaginable horrors; unexplorable depths; and especially ancient evils, all abound. A few of the stories felt familiar (and a few I’d also just read in The Thing on the Doorstep and Other Weird Stories). After a while they all sort of run together … neurotic white men in creepy New England or New York settings ever so slowly working out problems with horrible and sometimes messy endings. (Women rarely figure in the tales, and when they do it’s generally as objects of pity, derision, or villainy.) <br /><br />Reading Lovecraft as an adult is sobering. Even given some wiggle room for hyperbole befitting the horror genre, the way he describes human races and classes is ugly and unworthy. It’s important to revisit such works though, and not to hide them away, because they are part of our culture, and their poisons are in us, and until we acknowledge that we cannot repair the damage.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11855510576447498605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2774784574" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2774784574?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Taylor" href="/user/show/21665837-edward-taylor">Edward Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21665837-edward-taylor?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21665837-edward-taylor?shelf=hpl">hpl</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2774784574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18349653077319948081">This book shows the transition from his (HPL) days of critique and fanboy tirades to his own place as the head of the church of weird fiction. All of these stories are in other books (as with all things Lovecraft) but collected here to show the progress of Howard as a writer. We also see the rise of the Cthulhu Mythos (or as he preferred: Yog Sothery) in tales such as "At the Mountains of Madness", "Dagon" and others. Again, if you are into the collected editions of HPL from Del Rey Books, then </span>
  <span id="freeText18349653077319948081" style="display:none">This book shows the transition from his (HPL) days of critique and fanboy tirades to his own place as the head of the church of weird fiction. All of these stories are in other books (as with all things Lovecraft) but collected here to show the progress of Howard as a writer. We also see the rise of the Cthulhu Mythos (or as he preferred: Yog Sothery) in tales such as "At the Mountains of Madness", "Dagon" and others. Again, if you are into the collected editions of HPL from Del Rey Books, then this is a good addition to your shelves. If you see it as just another in the hundreds of truly unnecessary tomes of collected Lovecraft books and yet another cash grab on the part of publishing houses to make a dollar off a man dead but with strange eons not truly gone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18349653077319948081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sIrRahcZwZs5iviR3bKIMDUhQcMFwEj3iUbOh0sWj0ih9ypW5L5RtEDK5RuoHsQCa8vwPzs3Zh9lmAUcx9sgXQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gKDev/EdYRa11XifV2bJxj+mvfMlVBaX6kv1g44iQjWR3SWDArrxOcyVZRUiyoX0YUwMDxujOH8GlT4YAu/tIA==')})
  }

For those who already know these tales, the poignant introduction is a window on the creator\'s mind. Reflecting back after 14 years, Barker writes:  I look at these pieces and I don\'t think the man who wrote them is alive in me anymore.... We are all our own graveyards I believe; we squat amongst the tombs of the people we were. If we\'re healthy, every day is a celebration, a Day of the Dead, in which we give thanks for the lives that we lived; and if we are neurotic we brood and mourn and wish that the past was still present. Reading these stories over, I feel a little of both. Some of the simple energies that made these words flow through my pen--that made the phrases felicitous and the ideas sing--have gone. I lost their maker a long time ago.  These enthusiastic tales are not ashamed of visceral horror, of blood splashing freely across the page: &quot;The Midnight Meat Train,&quot; a grisly subway tale that surprises you with one twist after another; &quot;The Yattering and Jack,&quot; about a hilarious demon who possesses a Christmas turkey; &quot;In the Hills, the Cities,&quot; an unusual example of an original horror premise; &quot;Dread,&quot; a harrowing non-supernatural tale about being forced to realize your worst nightmare; &quot;Jacqueline Ess: Her Will and Testament,&quot; about a woman who kills men with her mind. Some of the tales are more successful than others, but all are distinguished by strikingly beautiful images of evil and destruction. No horror library is complete without them. --Fiona Webster<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9382492073038466600\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft">About H.P. Lovecraft</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
